Cyber Security (CYBER);

CRA;

Cybersecurity requirements for boot managers

HARMONISED EUROPEAN STANDARD

```
Exercising one of the Principles of International Standardization – Openness – and taking them to a different level, ETSI
CYBER-EUSR has conducted open consultations on the vertical standards in support of the Cyber-Resilience Act, at a much
earlier stage than it is usual in the standardization world. In this context, please keep in mind that the present document is an
INTERIM draft, which expectably will be subject to substantial changes before their target publication date in the second
semester of 2026.
ETSI CRA vertical standards v1.1.1 are being finalized and undergoing Public Enquiry via the National Standardization
Organizations (NSO). Therefore, from now on, ETSI CYBER-EUSR may only be able to address your comments in a future
revision of the standard. To enable ETSI CYBER-EUSR to address your comments before the first publication of the
standards, you should cumulatively submit to your NSO any comments submitted here. If you don't know how to do this,
email us at cybersupport@etsi.org and we will guide you in the process.
```
```
Disclaimer: The INTERIM DRAFTS available for download in this page are provided for information and are for future
development work within the ETSI Technical Committee CYBER EUSR Working Group (CYBER-EUSR) only. ETSI and its
Members accept no liability for any further use/implementation of these specifications. Approved and published specifications
and reports shall be obtained exclusively via the ETSI Documentation Service at http://www.etsi.org/standards-search
```
```
Commenting guidelines: Your comments to improve this early draft are very welcomed. To ensure the effectiveness of your
contribution, please make sure to include the following elements in your comment:
```
1. Clear identification of the section of the draft your comment is referring to.
2. Objective proposal to delete content, add content or substitute content.
3. Concrete contribution (exact content you suggest including in the draft as an addition to, or in substitution of, existing
    content).
4. Brief rationale to justify the proposal (e.g. why the suggested content should be added an/or the existing content should be
    deleted or substituted).
Please note that comments without concrete contributions will be noted but not acted upon by ETSI CYBER-EUSR. We trust
and value your expertise and therefore expect you to take this opportunity to proactively contribute to solving the issues you
find while analysing this early draft.
Use of Artificial Intelligence (AI): Please do not use large language models to generate your comments. Inclusion of language
generated by “AI” creates a potential for intellectual property conflicts and liability for ETSI, which is not acceptable.

```
How to comment: To comment or provide feedback on the present interim draft please visit: STAN4CRA / EN 304 623 Boot
Managers · GitLab and submit your comments as “issues” following the guidelines provided on this site. Alternatively, you may
also send your comments to: cybersupport@etsi.org using the “ETSI Commenting Format for Open Consultation.xlsx”
available in https://docbox.etsi.org/CYBER/EUSR/Open
Feedback on your comments: The resolutions made in Consensus by ETSI CYBER-EUSR members, on each comment
received through these Open Consultations will be published at the ETSI Open Area and ETSI Lab, assuring full
Transparency.
```

508 Modal verbs terminology

509 In the present document "shall", "shall not", "should", "should not", "may", "need not", "will", "will not", "can" and
510 "cannot" are to be interpreted as described in clause 3.2 of the ETSI Drafting Rules (Verbal forms for the expression of
511 provisions).

512 "must" and "must not" are NOT allowed in ETSI deliverables except when used in direct citation.

513 Introduction

514 The present document is a European harmonised standard that defines technical cybersecurity requirements for boot
515 managers as products with digital elements according to Regulation (EU) 2024/2847 [i.1], the Cyber Resilience Act
516 (CRA).

517 How to use the present document

518 Clause 4 describes the product context: architecture, capabilities, operational environment, and use cases. This clause is
519 informative and contains no requirements.

520 Clause 4.6 defines three use cases for boot manager products (UC-IMM, UC-VER and UC-HW), each associated with a
521 security profile (LOW, MEDIUM or HIGH).

522 The manufacturer declares the use case matching the product's capabilities as specified in Table 4.6.4-1.

523 Clause 5 contains the normative technical requirements. Requirements apply based on the declared security profile and
524 on the functional capabilities the product implements. Clause 5.1 specifies the applicability rules.

525 Clause 6 defines assessment criteria for each requirement.

526 Annex A maps requirements in the present document to CRA essential requirements.

527 Annex B documents the security analysis underlying the requirements.

528 Annex C contains information on the product documentation.

529 Annex D describes the relation to physical attack resistance.

530 Annex E provides an informative mapping to NIST SP 800-193 [i.10]

531 Annex K defines generic requirements and assessment criteria for the use of state-of-the-art cryptography. This Annex
532 is normative.

533


534 1 Scope

535 1.1 General

536 The present document specifies technical cybersecurity product requirements and corresponding assessment criteria for
537 boot managers. The products with digital elements in scope, there after "the products":

538  are specified within the technical description of the category of product number 8 by the Commission
539 Implementing Regulation (EU) 2025/2392 [i.2] as:

540 - "Software products with digital elements that manage the process of initial system startup after power
541 on/restart by initialising hardware, loading or transferring control to the operating system environment or
542 system resources, and selecting boot options. This category includes but is not limited to UEFI firmware,
543 single-stage and multi-stage boot loaders."

544  are only covered within the product context described in clause 4.

545 The scope covers software and firmware components that manage the boot process from power-on through
546 establishment of the chain of trust to handoff to the boot target. Products in scope include boot management software
547 and firmware regardless of distribution model or integration level. These are:

548  System firmware that performs hardware initialisation and boot management.

549  Bootloaders that manage boot target selection, verification, and loading.

550  Embedded boot firmware in IoT and embedded devices.

551  Network boot implementations enabling remote boot capabilities.

552  Boot managers that integrate with hardware security components for chain of trust establishment.

553 NOTE 1: Boot managers may be single-stage (direct loading) or multi-stage (staged verification).

554 NOTE 2: For microcontrollers (MCUs) and microprocessors (MPUs):

555  Silicon-integrated immutable firmware: Mask ROM, fused code, or boot firmware integrated during chip
556 manufacturing is assessed as part of MCU/MPU hardware under semiconductor standards.

557  Updateable boot managers: Boot software in flash storage (including OTP programmed post-manufacture) is
558 assessed using the present document when distinctly identifiable or independently updatable.

559 NOTE 3: Runtime services executing after boot target handoff (such as secure monitor mode handlers or attestation
560 services) are in scope only if they provide verification or attestation services to the boot process itself, not
561 to the boot target.

562 The present document covers those products to demonstrate compliance with essential cybersecurity requirements in the
563 Regulation (EU) 2024/2847 [i.1], Annex I Part I under the conditions identified in Annex A.

624 3 Definition of terms, symbols and abbreviations

625 3.1 Terms

626 For the purposes of the present document, the terms given in Regulation (EU) 2024/2847 [i.1] and the following apply:

627 attestation: process of providing cryptographic evidence about boot state, configuration, and measurements to a local
628 or remote verifier

629 NOTE: Attestation enables trust decisions based on demonstrated boot integrity without necessarily preventing
630 execution of unverified code.

631 EXAMPLE: Attestation may include measurement logs, signed attestation statements, and cryptographic proofs
632 of boot component identity

633 authorised entity: person, system, or process permitted to perform security-relevant operations on the boot manager,
634 where the permission depends on the lifecycle phase and the configured access control policy

635 NOTE 1: An authorised entity may be a manufacturer, integrator, administrator, or end user depending on the
636 lifecycle phase.

637 NOTE 2: Security-relevant operations may include configuration changes, trust anchor modification, update
638 installation, and recovery mode activation.

639 boot component: any executable code, firmware module, configuration data, or cryptographic material loaded and used
640 during the boot process

641 boot device: any storage medium or interface from which boot code can be loaded and executed, including internal
642 storage, removable media, or network sources

643 boot manager: software or firmware component that controls the boot process and allows management of multiple
644 boot targets or boot configurations after power-on or reset

645 NOTE: This term includes both single-purpose bootloaders and multi-function firmware with boot management
646 capabilities.

647 boot sequence: ordered series of operations from power-on through hardware initialisation, boot manager execution,
648 and handoff to the boot target

649 boot source: logical origin from which boot code can be loaded and executed

650 EXAMPLE: Boot devices, network interfaces, and debug interfaces.

651 NOTE: Each source presents different security boundaries and threat exposures.


652 boot target: software component to which the boot manager transfers control upon successful completion of its
653 designated function, such as an operating system kernel, hypervisor, another bootloader, or an embedded application

654 certificate: data structure that associates a public key with an identity, optionally including usage constraints, validity
655 period, and other attributes

656 NOTE: A certificate may be signed by a certification authority or may be self-signed. In the context of the present
657 document, "certificate" refers to both ITU-T X.509-compliant certificates and minimal public key
658 serialisations, as specified in the relevant requirements.

659 chain of trust: sequential verification where each boot component validates the next, also known as transitive trust or
660 trust chain, establishing security from hardware root to the boot target

661 critical data: data whose corruption would prevent successful boot completion or compromise security verification,
662 including boot code, verification keys, anti-rollback counters, and security policy settings

663 cryptographic mechanism: security-related procedure using cryptography

664 NOTE 1: The term "cryptographic mechanism" is used in this annex as an umbrella term covering the categories
665 used in the ECCG Agreed Cryptographic Mechanisms (ACM) catalogue [reference], including
666 cryptographic algorithms, primitives, schemes, protocols, protocol profiles, cipher suites, modes of
667 operation, constructions and parameter sets.

668 NOTE 2: The terms "cryptographic primitive", "cryptographic construction", "cryptographic scheme" and
669 "cryptographic protocol" are used consistently with the ECCG Agreed Cryptographic Mechanisms
670 (ACM) catalogue [reference].

671 NOTE 3: A cryptographic mechanism can be specified at different levels of abstraction. For example, AES is a
672 cryptographic primitive, AES-GCM is a mode of operation / authenticated encryption construction, TLS
673 1.3 is a protocol, a TLS 1.3 restricted cipher-suite profile is a protocol profile, and
674 TLS_AES_128_GCM_SHA256 is a cipher suite.

675 cryptographic property: property provided or supported by a cryptographic mechanism

676 NOTE 1: Cryptographic properties include, for example, confidentiality, integrity, authenticity, entity
677 authentication, message authentication, key establishment, key confirmation, replay protection, collision
678 resistance, second-preimage resistance, preimage resistance, signature unforgeability, non-repudiation,
679 forward secrecy and password-verifier protection. Where relevant, a cryptographic property can be
680 expressed using a formal cryptographic notion, for example IND-CPA, IND-CCA, IND-CCA2,
681 EUF-CMA, SUF-CMA or authenticated key exchange security.

682 NOTE 2: A cryptographic property is distinct from a product-level technical requirement, although it can support
683 such a requirement. For example, a secure communication requirement can rely on cryptographic
684 properties such as confidentiality, integrity and authentication.

685 defence-in-depth: security strategy employing multiple, overlapping, and diverse protection mechanisms so that
686 compromise of any single mechanism does not result in complete security failure

687 NOTE: In the present document, "threat" is used synonymously with "cyber threat" as defined in Regulation (EU)
688 2024/2847 [i.1].

689 enforcement: active prevention of unauthorised actions through cryptographic verification and blocking of execution
690 when verification fails, as distinct from detection-only approaches that record but do not prevent execution

691 essential operation: operation required for the boot manager to complete its primary function of loading and
692 transferring control to a boot target

693 evaluation activity: assessment procedure addressing a group of related requirements

694 firmware: low-level software stored in non-volatile memory on the device/product that provides hardware initialisation
695 and runtime services, including boot managers

696 firmware runtime services: functions provided by boot manager firmware that remain accessible to the operating
697 system after boot handoff, typically including variable storage, time services, and system management functions


698 handoff: transfer of execution control from the boot manager to the boot target, marking the completion of the boot
699 manager's function and the beginning of boot target execution

700 hardware security component: generic term for hardware-based security modules that provide cryptographic services,
701 secure storage, or isolated execution environments

702 NOTE 1: TPM and HSM are examples of hardware security components. The generic term 'hardware security
703 component' is used throughout the present document unless referring to specific implementation
704 examples.

705 NOTE 2: The term also includes immutable storage and protection provided by code-signed firmware (for example
706 trust anchors embedded in mask ROM or in write-protected firmware regions whose authenticity is
707 verifiable), where the integrity and where applicable confidentiality properties of such storage are
708 equivalent to those of dedicated hardware security modules.

709 immutable component: component whose code cannot be modified after manufacture or initial programming,
710 typically implemented in Read-Only Memory (ROM), One-Time Programmable (OTP) storage, e-fuses, or
711 write-protected flash memory

712 measured boot: recording cryptographic measurements of boot components before execution for attestation without
713 blocking execution

714 monotonic counter: counter value that can only increase, never decrease, used for anti-rollback protection to make
715 version downgrade detectable

716 network boot: loading boot components from network sources using protocols such as PXE or HTTPS Boot

717 physical presence: requirement for a user to be physically at the device location with direct physical access to the
718 device to perform certain security-sensitive operations, typically verified through local keyboard input or button presses

719 NOTE: Physical presence can also encompass operations conducted in a controlled environment such as a
720 manufacturing plant.

721 recovery boot: specialised boot process designed to restore system functionality after failures

722 rollback protection: security mechanism preventing unauthenticated installation of older firmware versions that may
723 contain known vulnerabilities

724 root of trust: component providing foundational trust for the system through hardware-protected execution and storage

725 NOTE: Root of Trust is typically implemented in immutable ROM, hardware security modules, or physically
726 isolated processors. Serves as the anchor for chain of trust verification.

727 secure boot: specific UEFI implementation of verified boot as defined in the UEFI Specification [i.14]

728 security-critical configuration: configuration settings whose modification would reduce security posture, including
729 verification enable/disable settings, trust anchor databases, certificates, user-enrolled keys, boot order, and recovery
730 mode settings

731 sensitive data: data requiring protection from disclosure

732 EXAMPLE: Sensitive data include cryptographic private keys and symmetric keys.

733 NOTE: Unauthenticated access to sensitive data enables impersonation, forgery, or decryption of protected
734 content.

735 trust anchor: public key, certificate, or cryptographic hash that serves as the starting point for verification and is
736 trusted without requiring further validation

737 update capability: ability to modify boot manager code, configuration, or security policies after initial deployment,
738 ranging from configuration-only updates to full firmware replacement


739 verified boot: cryptographic verification of boot components before execution to ensure only authorised code runs
740 during system initialisation

741 NOTE: Implementations verify authenticity using cryptographic signatures or authenticated hashes bound to
742 cryptographic keys or hardware roots of trust.

744 Void.

820 4 Product context

821 4.1 Product Functions

822 4.1.1 Intended purpose

823 Boot managers establish initial system trust and manage the boot process from power-on through handoff to the boot
824 target. They serve as the foundational trust component that executes before operating systems or security services are
825 available.

826 The intended purpose includes:

827  Establishing the initial chain of trust for the system.

828  Loading and transferring control to boot targets (such as another bootloader, operating systems, hypervisors, or
829 embedded applications).

830  Managing boot configuration and security policy.

831 Where capable it also includes:

832  Verifying integrity of boot components.

833  Authentication of boot components.

834  Recording measurements for attestation.

835  Providing recovery mechanisms.


836 4.1.2 Reasonably foreseeable use

837 Boot managers are components that may be:

838  Integrated into diverse hardware platforms across all industry verticals.

839  Deployed in security contexts ranging from consumer devices to critical infrastructure.

840  Configured by users with varying levels of technical expertise.

841  Operated in environments ranging from physically secure to hostile and untrusted.

842  Transferred to secondary markets without factory reset.

843  Operated beyond the manufacturers active support lifecycle.

844  Combined with other components in composite products.

845  Subjected to configuration changes by downstream integrators.

846  Used in safety-critical systems where availability requirements may conflict with security update procedures.

847 These reasonably foreseeable uses inform the requirements in clause 5 and risk analysis methodology in Annex B.

848 Reasonably foreseeable deployment contexts determine risk level determination as described in clause B.2. Boot
849 managers are commonly composed of components from multiple sources including silicon vendors, independent BIOS
850 vendors, OEMs, and open-source projects. Each component may have distinct update mechanisms, vulnerability
851 response capabilities, and maintenance lifecycles.

852 4.1.3 Reasonably foreseeable misuse

853 Reasonably foreseeable misuse includes use of the boot manager in ways not intended but predictable from human
854 behaviour or system interactions. Examples include:

855  Disabling security features for convenience

856  Using development/debug modes in production

857  Operating without applying available security updates

858  Connecting to untrusted networks or storage

859 Requirements in clause 5 address these scenarios through secure defaults and fail-safe behaviour.

860 4.1.4 General functions

861 Boot managers establish system trust and load the boot target. This process includes verifying or measuring boot
862 components, managing boot configuration, and transferring execution control to the selected target.

863 Security functions vary by design. Verified boot implementations block execution of unverified code through
864 cryptographic signature verification. Measured boot implementations record cryptographic measurements of boot
865 components for attestation purposes. Both approaches protect boot configuration from unauthorised modification and
866 may include rollback protection against version downgrade attacks.

867 Configuration capabilities determine how boot behaviour can be modified. Some implementations have fixed behaviour
868 determined at manufacturing. Others allow modification of security policies during subsequent boots, cryptographic
869 credentials, and boot target selection. Boot managers with configuration capability implement access controls to prevent
870 unauthorised changes.

871 Lifecycle management includes update, recovery, and reset functions. Updates modify boot manager firmware or
872 configuration post-deployment. Recovery functions provide alternative boot paths when primary operations fail. Reset
873 capabilities support secure decommissioning by erasing cryptographic material and restoring default configurations,
874 where technically feasible. However, when cryptographic material or configuration data are stored in immutable
875 components such as e-fuses or ROM, complete erasure or restoration may not be possible.


876 4.2 Product Architecture

877 4.2.1 Boot architecture

878 4.2.1.1 Unverified boot

879 Unverified Boot implements sequential stage execution without cryptographic verification mechanisms.

880 The system begins with power-on, proceeds through hardware initialisation, and continues with sequential stage
881 execution where each stage directly loads and executes the next until the boot target is reached.

882 Unverified boot implements no cryptographic authentication mechanisms, but includes simple integrity checks such as
883 CRC, checksums or hashes. These mechanisms may detect accidental corruption but do not provide protection against
884 deliberate modification or substitution attacks.

885 Unverified boot is appropriate for environments where physical security controls provide the primary protection, or for
886 device categories (e.g. sensors) where risk assessment determines that the assets stored or transmitted do not warrant
887 verified boot implementation.

### 888

889 Figure 4.2.1.1-1

890 4.2.1.2 Verified boot

891 Verified boot implements cryptographic verification with enforcement throughout the boot process. Components are
892 cryptographically verified before execution, creating a verification chain from power-on through boot target execution.

893 NOTE: Verification may be performed either immediately before execution (on-boot verification) or prior to
894 installation with integrity-protected storage retained between updates (on-update verification, also
895 referred to as capsule update). Both patterns establish that the code executed on the device has been
896 cryptographically verified at some point in time before its execution. A boot manager may implement
897 either pattern or a combination of both.

898 Components are verified against stored cryptographic signatures or authenticated hash values and if verification fails the
899 system halts the boot process and potentially triggers recovery mode. Initial verification material (keys or hashes) may
900 be stored in hardware-protected memory, fuses, or one-time programmable storage. Systems implementing verified
901 boot need cryptographic verification capabilities and secure storage for verification material. Verified boot employs
902 cryptographic authentication mechanisms using either:

903  Asymmetric cryptography: Digital signatures verified using public keys stored in the trust root (e.g. RSA,
904 ECDSA, EdDSA signatures)

905  Symmetric cryptography: Message Authentication Codes (MACs) or authenticated encryption verified against
906 pre-shared secrets (e.g. HMAC, CMAC, AES-GCM, KMAC)

907  One-way functions, such as cryptographic hash functions, which are a necessary component in signature
908 systems, and map data and public keys to protected trust anchors

909 NOTE: See Annex K for approved cryptographic mechanisms and deprecation schedules.

910 When correctly applied, either together or separately, these mechanisms provide cryptographic assurance that boot
911 components have not been modified and originate from a trusted source. When verification fails, the system halts


912 execution and may transition to a degraded secure state, trigger recovery mode, or perform a power-reset to prevent
913 execution of unauthorised code.

914 The choice between asymmetric and symmetric authentication schemes, including the use of authenticated hashes and
915 digital signatures, depends on the threat model, key management capabilities, available cryptographic hardware support,
916 and performance requirements particularly in safety-critical contexts where boot time is constrained.

### 917

918 Figure 4.2.1.2-1

919 4.2.1.3 Measured boot

920 Measured boot records cryptographic measurements of each boot stage without preventing execution. During
921 initialisation, the system activates measurement capabilities and records a cryptographic hash of each subsequent stage
922 into secure storage.

923 Each stage computes a cryptographic hash of the next component and stores or extends it into designated registers, and
924 the boot process continues regardless of measurement values. The resulting measurement log provides an attestable
925 record of the boot sequence that can be verified against known-good values or reference measurements.

926 This architecture relies on hardware or firmware capable of secure measurement process, storage and reporting. The
927 fundamental difference from verified boot is in the enforcement policy. The system records measurements in the log but
928 continues the boot process regardless of values. This allows:

929  Attestation of the actual boot state to remote verifiers.

930  Post-boot analysis and remediation decisions.

931  Operation in degraded mode with logged anomalies.

932  Flexibility for development and recovery scenarios.

933  Execution of parts of the system software conditional on the measured stages being valid.

934 The measurement log provides a cryptographically protected, auditable record that can be verified locally or remotely
935 against known-good reference values or security policies.

936 Measured boot employs the same cryptographic authentication mechanisms as verified boot.

937 EXAMPLE: The diagram below illustrates the general measured boot architecture.

938 Each boot stage computes a cryptographic hash and records it into hardware-protected storage (hardware-based Root of
939 Trust), which may be implemented using a variety of technologies including PCRs in a TPM [i.15], CDI derivation in
940 DICE [i.16] and [i.17], or other secure measurement storage mechanisms.


### 941

942 Figure 4.2.1.3-1

943 4.2.2 Functional extensions

944 Boot managers may provide additional functions beyond core boot architecture. These functions are classified as either
945 security-enhancing or functional (see clause 4.2.3).

946 Security-enhancing capabilities improve the security posture of the boot manager and characterise the use cases defined
947 in clause 4.6.

948 Functional capabilities provide features that may be required by the product's intended purpose but introduce additional
949 attack surface. Where a functional capability is implemented, the requirements in clause 5 that reference that capability
950 apply. Both types of capability can be combined with any boot architecture (unverified, verified, or measured).

951 4.2.3 Product capabilities

952 4.2.3.1 Introduction

953 The following clauses describe each capability. Table 4.2.3.1-1 summarises the classification defined in clause 4.2.2.
954 Where applicability statements in clause 5 refer to boot managers with a particular capability, they apply to boot
955 managers implementing any option listed in the corresponding clause other than the "no [capability]" option, unless the
956 clause defines a different threshold.

957 Table 4.2.3.1-1: Capability classification

Security-enhancing Functional
Verified boot (clause 4. 2 .1.2) Network boot (clause 4. 2 .3.3)
Update capability (clause 4. 2 .3.2) Configuration management (clause 4. 2 .3. 4 )
Logging capability (clause 4.2.3.7) Measured boot (clause 4.2.1.3)
Hardware security (clause 4. 2 .3.5) Recovery capability (clause 4. 2 .3.6)
Key provisioning (clause 4.2.3.10) Authentication capability (clause 4.2.3.8)
Debug interface (clause 4. 2 .3.9)
958

959 4.2.3.2 Update capability

960 Update capability describes whether and how the boot manager can be modified after initial deployment.

961  No updates: Boot manager code and configuration cannot be modified after manufacture.

962  Configuration updates: Only boot configuration, security policies, and trusted keys can be updated. Boot
963 manager code remains immutable.

964  Partial updates: Specific components can be updated while core components remain immutable. Root of trust
965 and initial boot stages cannot be modified.


```
966  Full updates: Complete boot manager firmware can be updated including all boot stages.
```
```
967  Runtime updates: Boot manager can be updated while the system is running without requiring reboot until
968 the next boot cycle.
```
```
969  Recovery-mode updates: Updates can only be applied through a separate recovery mode or alternate boot
970 path.
```
```
971  Network updates: Boot manager can fetch and apply updates directly from network sources.
```
```
972  Enclave-managed updates: Updates are managed by a separate security processor or trusted execution
973 environment.
```
```
974 Products may combine multiple update capabilities (e.g. configuration updates via network, full updates via recovery
975 mode), except products with no update capability. For the purposes of the present document, references to "update
976 capability" in clause 5 apply to boot managers implementing partial updates, full updates, runtime updates, recovery-
977 mode updates, network updates, or enclave-managed updates. Boot managers implementing only "no updates" or
978 "configuration updates" are not considered to have update capability.
```
979 4.2.3.3 Boot source

```
980 Boot source identifies media types from which the boot manager can load and execute code.
```
```
981  Internal storage: Boot exclusively from non-volatile storage integrated within the same package or die as the
982 processor.
```
```
983  Fixed storage: Boot exclusively from non-removable storage physically attached to the system but in a
984 separate package.
```
```
985  Removable storage: Can boot from removable storage devices such as USB, SD cards, or optical media.
```
```
986  Network boot: Can boot from network sources including PXE, HTTP, iSCSI, or cloud services.
```
```
987  External expansion: Can boot from external expansion buses such as Thunderbolt or ExpressCard.
```
```
988  Debug interfaces: Can boot or load code via debug or programming interfaces such as JTAG, SWD, or
989 UART.
```
```
990 Multiple boot sources are common. Each additional source increases the attack surface.
```
```
991 NOTE: Requirements referencing "Network boot" apply when the boot manager includes Network boot
992 capability. Requirements referencing "Network update" apply when the boot manager includes Network
993 updates under update capability. These may overlap but are distinct: Network boot loads the entire boot
994 image from network while network update modifies persistent boot manager components.
```
995 4.2.3.4 Configuration management

```
996 Configuration capability describes whether boot behaviour can be modified through settings.
```
```
997  No configuration: Fixed behaviour with no configurable settings, or configuration set once via one-time
998 programmable mechanisms.
```
```
999  Static configuration: Configuration can be set but involves physical access or special tools to modify.
```
1000  Local configuration: Configuration modifiable through local interfaces requiring physical presence.

1001  Protected configuration: Configuration modifiable with authentication but without physical presence
1002 requirement.

1003  Remote configuration: Configuration modifiable through network interfaces.

1004  Runtime configuration: Configuration modifiable by the operating system through runtime services.


1005 For the purposes of the present document, references to "configuration capability" in clause 5 apply to boot managers
1006 implementing local, protected, remote, or runtime configuration. Boot managers implementing only "no configuration"
1007 or "static configuration" are not considered to have configuration capability.

1008 4.2.3.5 Hardware security

1009 Hardware security describes whether the boot manager utilises dedicated security hardware.

1010 Software only: Boot manager operates entirely in software without dedicated security hardware.

1011 Hardware storage: Utilises hardware components for protected key storage and cryptographic operations.

1012 Hardware enforcement: Hardware actively enforces security policies during boot.

1013 Hardware enforcement typically includes hardware storage.

1014 4.2.3.6 Recovery capability

1015 Recovery capability describes how the system can recover from boot failures, corruption, or attacks.

1016  No recovery: No separate recovery path or fallback boot mechanism.

1017  Basic recovery: Simple recovery mode or fallback boot option such as safe mode or previous known-good
1018 configuration.

1019  Full recovery: Comprehensive recovery environment with diagnostics, repair tools, and restore capabilities.

1020 Recovery mechanisms provide resilience but introduce additional attack surface.

1021 4.2.3.7 Logging capability

1022 Logging capability describes whether and how the boot manager can record, store, and communicate security-relevant
1023 events and operational states during the boot process. The presence, type, and durability of logging functions depend on
1024 the boot managers architecture, resource constraints, and deployment context.

1025  No logging: The boot manager does not implement any logging functionality. No events or states are recorded
1026 during boot in persistent storage.

1027 NOTE 1: This variant is typical for resource-constrained devices or early boot stages where logging infrastructure
1028 is not available.

1029  Volatile logging: The boot manager records events in volatile memory during boot. Logs are lost upon power-
1030 cycle or reset and are accessible only during the current boot session.

1031 NOTE 2: Requirements referring to persistent log retrieval cannot be fulfilled when only volatile logging is
1032 implemented.

1033  Persistent logging: The boot manager records events in persistent storage (e.g. flash, NVRAM, dedicated log
1034 partition). Logs survive power cycles and can be accessed after reboot.

1035  Hardware-protected logging: The boot manager authorises hardware security components (e.g. TPM, secure
1036 element, write-once memory) to protect log integrity and prevent tampering.

1037  External logging: The boot manager exports log events to external interfaces (e.g. serial console, debug ports)
1038 for real-time monitoring or remote analysis.

1039 Logging capability enables security monitoring and forensic analysis but uses storage resources.

1040 4.2.3.8 Authentication capability

1041 Authentication capability describes whether the boot manager involves identity verification for privileged operations:

1042  No authentication: No identity verification for any operations.


1043  Physical presence: Operations require physical presence detection (button press, jumper) but no credential
1044 verification.

1045  Password authentication: Operations require password or PIN verification.

1046  Cryptographic authentication: Operations require cryptographic credential verification (smart card,
1047 certificate, hardware token).

1048  Multi-factor authentication: Operations require multiple authentication mechanisms.

1049 Authentication capability protects configuration and security policy changes from unauthorised modification.

1050 4.2.3.9 Debug interface

1051 Debug interface describes whether the boot manager exposes diagnostic or programming interfaces.

1052  No debug interface: No debug, diagnostic, or programming interfaces present in production.

1053  Disabled debug: Debug interfaces physically present but permanently disabled or fused off.

1054  Authenticated debug: Debug interfaces require authentication before activation.

1055  Conditional debug: Debug interfaces available only in specific modes (e.g. manufacturing, recovery).

1056  Open debug: Debug interfaces accessible without restriction.

1057 Debug interfaces enable development and diagnostics but significantly increase attack surface when accessible. For the
1058 purposes of the present document, references to "debug capability" in clause 5 apply to boot managers implementing
1059 authenticated, conditional, or open debug interfaces. Boot managers with no debug interface, or with permanently
1060 disabled debug interfaces, are not considered to have debug capability.

1061 4.2.3.10 Key provisioning

1062 Key provisioning describes whether and how trust anchors used for boot verification can be provisioned by the
1063 integrator of the boot manager.

1064  No key provisioning: Trust anchors are fixed at manufacture and cannot be modified.

1065  Manufacturing provisioning: Trust anchors are provisioned during manufacturing or integration using
1066 dedicated tools or interfaces.

1067  Updateable provisioning: Trust anchors can be modified post-deployment through authenticated
1068 mechanisms.

1069 Key provisioning does not imply that end users can modify trust anchors. It describes a mechanism available to the
1070 integrator. Whether the integrator exposes trust anchor management to downstream entities is outside the scope of the
1071 present document.

1072 4.2.4 Resource constraints

1073 Boot managers operate under inherent resource limitations determined by their implementation and hardware platform:

1074  Storage: Restricted firmware storage capacity shared with other components.

1075  Computation: No OS services, drivers, or shared libraries during early stages.

1076  Time: Boot performance requirements limiting security operations.

1077  Memory: Limited RAM available during early boot stages.

1078 Resource constraints influence implementation choices and inform requirement applicability.


1079 4.3 Operational environment

1080 4.3.1 Introduction

1081 The operational environment describes deployment context affecting risk factor assessment described in clause B.2.
1082 Environmental characteristics are independent of product characteristics and describe where and how the boot manager
1083 is deployed.

1084 4.3.2 Physical security context

1085  Secure: Physically secure locations (data centres, secure facilities).

1086  Controlled: Controlled access environments (offices, industrial sites).

1087  Restricted: Limited public access with some monitoring (hospitals, retail).

1088  Public: Publicly accessible with minimal security (kiosks, ATMs, digital signage).

1089  Hostile: Uncontrolled, adversarial environments (consumer devices, field deployment).

1090  Mobile: Travels through varying physical environments (laptops, smartphones, tablets - should assume
1091 hostile).

1092 4.3.3 Network exposure

1093 Boot managers may be deployed with varying levels of network exposure:

1094  None (air-gapped): No network connectivity.

1095  Isolated: Private/isolated networks (LAN, industrial networks).

1096  Managed: Internet-connected within a managed environment (VPN, enterprise).

1097  Internet: Direct internet exposure.

1098  Cellular: Mobile/cellular networks.

1099 4.3.4 Data sensitivity and value

1100 The sensitivity and value of data accessible through or protected by the boot manager affects attacker motivation and
1101 consequence severity:

1102  Minimal: No significant sensitive data (basic sensors, simple IoT).

1103  Personal: Personal user data (consumer devices, personal files).

1104  Financial: Financial/payment data (payment terminals).

1105  Business: Business confidential data (enterprise systems, intellectual property).

1106  Health: Protected health information (medical devices, health records).

1107  Critical: Critical infrastructure control (power grid, water systems).

1108  Classified: Government classified information.

1109 4.3.5 System criticality

1110 Impact severity if the boot manager is compromised, considering availability, integrity, and safety:

1111  Non-critical: Low business impact, non-critical functions, general purpose devices.


1112  Business impact: Moderate business impact, internal systems, productivity loss, reputation damage.

1113  Safety-critical: Systems where compromise causes physical harm, critical infrastructure disruption, major
1114 financial loss or widespread service disruption.

1115 4.3.6 Lifecycle expectations

1116  Short: < 5 years.

1117  Medium: 5 to 15 years.

1118  Long: > 15 years.

1119 4.3.7 Administrative control

1120 The model for managing configuration and updates:

1121  Centrally-managed: IT department or security operations centre controls all configuration and updates,
1122 security policies are centrally enforced, compliance monitoring is in place.

1123  User-managed: Individual end-users control configuration and updates. Varying security expertise,
1124 inconsistent update adoption.

1125  Vendor-managed: Manufacturer retains control over updates and configuration. Users cannot modify boot
1126 security settings.

1127  Hybrid-managed: Shared responsibility between multiple parties, needs clearly documented responsibility
1128 delineation.

1129  Autonomous: No administrative interface, device operates independently. Updates require physical access or
1130 hardware replacement.

1131 4.3.8 Availability requirements

1132  Flexible: Downtime acceptable for updates/maintenance.

1133  Standard: Normal business availability expectations.

1134  High: High uptime required.

1135  Critical: Continuous operation required for safety or critical infrastructure.

1136 4.3.9 Additional environmental characteristics

1137 4.3.9.1 Environmental stress

1138 Harsh environmental conditions affect boot manager reliability and security:

1139  Temperature extremes: Harsh thermal environments affecting non-volatile memory reliability and boot
1140 component integrity.

1141  Vibration and shock: Mechanical stress causing storage connection failures and data corruption risks.

1142  Radiation: Environments with ionising radiation causing storage bit flips requiring error correction and
1143 redundancy.

1144  Humidity and corrosion: Outdoor deployment, marine environments affecting hardware integrity.

1145  Aging effects: Flash wear (limited write cycles), bit rot, electromigration degradation over extended lifecycles.

1146 These stresses increase probability of boot failures, requiring enhanced error detection, redundant storage, and robust
1147 recovery mechanisms. See REQ-BM-AP-006 and REQ-BM-AP-007.


1148 4.3.9.2 Update frequency expectations

1149 Boot manager update patterns vary by deployment:

1150  Never: ROM-based, OTP firmware, no post-manufacture updates possible.

1151  Rare: Major version updates only (e.g. every 2 to 5 years) for long-lifecycle industrial equipment.

1152  Periodic: Scheduled maintenance windows (quarterly, annually) for enterprise/infrastructure.

1153  Responsive: Updates deployed when vulnerabilities are discovered, following a testing-dependent timeline.

1154  Continuous: Rapid update capability for internet-connected consumer devices.

1155 Update frequency affects vulnerability exposure window and influences defence-in-depth requirements and may also
1156 affect the lifetime of memory used. See clause B.2.2 (RF-SURFACE).

1157 4.3.9.3 Interfaces

1158 Boot managers interact through various technical interfaces:

1159  Storage: SPI flash, USB, eMMC, NVMe, SATA with different protection mechanisms.

1160  Network: Ethernet, WiFi supporting PXE, HTTP Boot, iSCSI protocols.

1161  Debug: JTAG, SWD, UART, USB, SPI programmers requiring access control.

1162  User: Serial console, display output, keyboard, remote management (IPMI/BMC).

1163  Hardware: Hardware security component connections (SPI/I2C or other protocols).

1164  Runtime: Firmware runtime services, ACPI tables, SMM/SMI interfaces.

1165 Boot managers receive inputs such as hardware reset signals, configuration data, boot media, network packets, or user
1166 input, and produce outputs including system state, measurement logs, or error codes. Control interfaces include
1167 configuration utilities, update mechanisms, recovery modes, and remote management protocols.

1168 The interface types listed above are comprehensive. The specific protocols and technologies listed under each type are
1169 representative examples; additional interfaces assignable to these types are subject to the same security considerations
1170 described in clause 5.

1171 4.4 Distribution of security functions

1172 4.4.1 Introduction

1173 As a component within a larger system, boot managers both provide and depend upon security functions. This clause
1174 clarifies the security boundaries and responsibilities.

1175 4.4.2 Security functions provided by the boot manager

1176 Depending on capabilities, boot managers provide:

1177  Initial trust establishment before any other software executes.

1178  Boot chain integrity verification.

1179  Cryptographic measurement recording for attestation.

1180  Secure handoff to boot target.

1181  Protection of boot configuration and policy.

1182  Rollback protection for boot components.


1183  Root of trust for measurement.

1184  Root of trust for verification.

1185  Root of trust for storage.

1186  Root of trust for reporting.

1187 NOTE: Verification, measurement, and rollback capabilities are implementation dependent.

1188 4.4.3 Security functions required from the platform

1189 The boot manager may depend on the following security functions from the hardware platform. These dependencies are
1190 documented as assumptions in clause B.3.2. Where a platform does not provide a listed function, the corresponding
1191 threats identified in clause B.3.2 are not mitigated by the present document.

1192  Hardware root of trust (immutable boot ROM or equivalent).

1193  Secure storage for cryptographic keys and configuration.

1194  Protected execution environment (where available).

1195  Hardware security components for key storage (where available).

1196  Physical security mechanisms (where available).

1197 NOTE: Hardware security mechanisms may include various implementations such as TPM, HSM, secure
1198 elements, or write protection mechanisms that prevent tampering of boot manager code by the operating
1199 system or other software executing after boot handoff.

1200 4.4.4 Security functions delegated to boot target

1201 The following security functions are outside the scope of the boot manager and are the responsibility of the loaded boot
1202 target:

1203  Runtime security monitoring and threat detection.

1204  User authentication and authorisation (post-boot).

1205  Network security and firewall functions.

1206  Application security and sandboxing.

1207  Ongoing vulnerability management and patching.

1208  Security event logging (post-handoff).

1209 NOTE: Some boot managers provide runtime services that remain accessible after boot target handoff (e.g. UEFI
1210 runtime services for variable storage, time, capsule updates or attestation). These runtime services are
1211 within scope when they affect boot security, integrity, or configuration.

1212 4.4.5 Trust boundaries

1213 Boot managers operate across multiple trust boundaries where different security domains interface.

1214 Boot managers interface with multiple trust domains, each requiring different verification approaches, for example root
1215 of trust by hardware vendor, security hashes for different stages by firmware or board OEM, or certificates by operating
1216 system vendors.

1217  Hardware: Components trusted by boot manager without verification capability, including CPU microcode,
1218 platform security processors providing opaque services, immutable boot ROM and/or e-fuses.


1219  Storage: Components requiring verification before use, including all data read from storage media,
1220 configuration data, and boot components that may have been tampered with or corrupted.

1221  Network: Untrusted input requiring complete verification, including all network-sourced boot components,
1222 protocol data, and remote configuration.

1223  Operating system: Components to which the boot manager transfers control, including operating systems,
1224 hypervisors, and runtime services. This boundary is bidirectional when the operating system performs updates
1225 to the boot manager.

1226 These boundaries define cases boot managers can enforce security properties or cases where they rely instead on trust
1227 assumptions or external security mechanisms.

1228 4.5 Users

1229 4.5.1 Introduction

1230 Boot managers operate mostly without any user interaction during normal operation, with security decisions
1231 predetermined by configuration rather than runtime user input. In the requirements of clause 5, "authorised entity" is
1232 used in place of "user" to avoid ambiguity between the CRA definition of user and technical access roles. See
1233 clause 3.1.

1234 4.5.2 User categories

1235 Boot managers interact with different user types across their lifecycle, including:

1236  Manufacturers, during production and initial provisioning.

1237  System integrators, during deployment.

1238  System administrators, for enterprise configuration.

1239  End users, for boot selection.

1240  Service technicians, during maintenance.

1241 4.5.3 User interaction patterns

1242  Manufacturing phase: Fuse programming, key provisioning, initial firmware installation, testing.

1243  Configuration phase: Use of setup and configuration tools.

1244 NOTE: In some business models, operations such as fuse programming or key provisioning may be deferred from
1245 the manufacturing phase to the configuration phase, allowing OEMs to customise or finalise device
1246 configuration post-manufacture.

1247  Boot phase: Physical presence detection, boot target selection, recovery mode activation.

1248  Maintenance phase: Updates, rollbacks, resets.

1249  Decommissioning phase: Secure erasure, memory clean-up, key destruction, decommissioning verification.

1250 These interactions represent points where boot manager security can be modified, compromised or bypassed, requiring
1251 appropriate access controls and authentication mechanisms.

1252 Interactions requiring special attention:

1253  Device resale or transfer.

1254  Return for service/warranty.

1255  Employee termination (enterprise devices).


1256  End-of-life disposal.

1257 These scenarios present opportunities for security policy changes, credential transfer, or secure erasure failures that
1258 could compromise subsequent users or violate data protection obligations.

1259 User interactions requiring authentication are addressed in clause 5.4. Physical presence verification provides protection
1260 for security-critical configuration changes.

1261 4.6 Use cases

1262 4.6.1 Purpose

1263 This clause describes use cases that characterise boot manager products by the type of security-enhancing capabilities
1264 provided.

1265 A boot manager manufacturer declares the use case that corresponds to the capabilities of their product as placed on the
1266 market. Each use case is associated with a security profile (LOW, MEDIUM, or HIGH); each security profile
1267 determines which requirements in clause 5 apply.

1268 Separately, boot manager products may implement functional capabilities (see clause 4.6.4) that are not associated with
1269 any specific use case but that, when present, trigger additional requirements in clause 5, serving to mitigate the risks
1270 introduced by those capabilities. The normative requirements for use case declaration and requirement application are
1271 specified in clause 5.1.

1272 4.6.2 Capabilities as risk exposures

1273 Most capabilities described in clause 4.2.3 do not inherently improve the security of the boot manager. They provide
1274 however functionality that may be required by the product's intended purpose. Each capability introduces additional
1275 attack surface and associated risks. A boot manager that does not implement a given capability is not subject to the risks
1276 introduced by the specific capability.

1277 Where a boot manager implements a functional capability, the requirements in clause 5 that reference that capability
1278 apply. These requirements mitigate the risks introduced by the capability. Their applicability is conditional on the
1279 capability being present, not on the use case or security profile.

1280 A limited set of capabilities genuinely enhance the security posture of the system. These capabilities are associated with
1281 the use cases defined in clause 4.6.3 because they establish the security foundation of the product. See Table 4.2.3.1-1
1282 for the classification of capabilities.

1283 4.6.3 Use case definitions

1284 4.6.3.0 Use cases and security profiles

1285 Three use cases are defined. Each use case is characterised by the security-enhancing capabilities the boot manager
1286 implements and is associated with a security profile. Use cases are cumulative: each higher use case includes all
1287 requirements of the use cases below it.

1288 The rationale for the association between use cases and security profiles is described in the present document
1289 (Annex B).

1290 Table 4.6.3-1: Use cases and security profiles

Use case Security profile Characterised by
UC-IMM LOW Immutable code + trust anchors
UC-VER MEDIUM Verified boot + updatable + logging +
key provisioning
UC-HW HIGH UC-VER + hardware-assisted security
1291


1292 4.6.3.1 UC-IMM: Immutable

1293 UC-IMM describes a boot manager where the executable code and trust anchors are stored in hardware-enforced
1294 immutable storage and cannot be modified after deployment.

1295 The associated security profile: for UC-IMM is: LOW. All requirements for security profile LOW apply.

1296 NOTE 1: The security posture of UC-IMM relies on immutability as the primary security property. The absence of
1297 update, configuration, and network boot capabilities eliminates entire classes of attack vectors.

1298 NOTE 2: A UC-IMM boot manager cannot be remediated if a vulnerability is discovered post-deployment.

1299 NOTE 3: UC-IMM addresses immutability of the boot manager only. Properties of the post-handoff stage are an
1300 environmental assumption (see clause B.3.4, AS-TARGET-2). Where this assumption does not hold, UC-
1301 IMM's security posture does not extend to the running system.

1302 4.6.3.2 UC-VER: Verified and updateable

1303 UC-VER describes a boot manager that implements verified boot, update capability, logging, and key provisioning as
1304 its security foundation.

1305 The associated security profile: for UC-VER is: MEDIUM. All requirements for security profiles LOW and MEDIUM
1306 apply.

1307 NOTE 1: The association of verified boot and update capability is deliberate: verified boot without update
1308 capability prevents remediation of compromised keys or signing infrastructure; update capability without
1309 verified boot provides no integrity assurance for updates.

1310 NOTE 2: Key provisioning is a capability of the boot manager product. Whether the device manufacturer in which
1311 the boot manager is integrated exposes key management to the device owner is a decision for the device
1312 manufacturer. The boot manager provides the mechanism; it does not determine downstream key
1313 management policy.

1314 4.6.3.3 UC-HW: Hardware-assisted security

1315 UC-HW describes a boot manager that implements all UC-VER capabilities plus hardware-assisted security
1316 mechanisms.

1317 The associated security profile: for UC-HW is: HIGH. All requirements for security profiles LOW, MEDIUM and
1318 HIGH apply.

1319 NOTE: UC-HW does not imply resistance to advanced physical attacks (e.g. side-channel analysis, fault
1320 injection, chip decapsulation).

1321 4.6.4 Functional capabilities and additional requirements

1322 Functional capabilities (see Table 4.2.3.1-1) are not associated with any specific use case. They may be implemented
1323 based on the product's intended purpose. Where a functional capability is implemented, all requirements in clause 5 that
1324 reference that capability become applicable, regardless of the declared use case. These requirements mitigate the risks
1325 introduced by the capability.


1326 Table 4.6.4-1: Capability requirements by use case

```
Capability UC-IMM UC-VER UC-HW
Verified boot (see note 1 ) Not expected Required Required
Update capability Not permitted Required Required
Logging Optional Required Required
Key provisioning Not expected
(see note 2)
```
```
Required Required
```
```
Hardware security Optional Optional Required
Network boot Not permitted
(see note 3)
```
```
Optional Optional
```
Configuration management Limited (see note 4) Optional Optional
Measured boot Not expected Optional Optional
Recovery Not expected Optional Optional
Authentication Optional Optional Optional
Debug interface Optional Optional Optional
NOTE 1: "Verified boot" covers both on-boot verification and on-update verification (capsule update)
as defined in clause 4.2.1.2. Individual requirements in clause 5 may apply specifically to on-
boot or on-update verification. Such scope is stated in the applicability statement of the
requirement.
NOTE 2: "Not expected" means the capability serves no purpose given the use case characterisation
but is not prohibited. If nevertheless implemented, the corresponding requirements apply.
NOTE 3: "Not permitted" means the capability is incompatible with the use case characterisation. A
boot manager implementing that capability cannot declare that use case.
NOTE 4: Under UC-IMM, configuration capability is limited to operational parameters that do not
affect the security properties of the boot manager.
1327

1328 4.6.5 Use case selection

1329 Use case selection is based on the security-enhancing capabilities the boot manager implements as placed on the
1330 market, as follows:

1331 a) UC-IMM, if the boot manager code, configuration, and trust anchors are immutable.

1332 b) UC-VER, if the boot manager implements verified boot, update capability, logging, and key provisioning:
1333 UC-VER.

1334 c) IC-HW, if the boot manager additionally uses hardware-backed security mechanisms: UC-HW.

1335 Where a boot manager implements some but not all capabilities of a higher use case, the manufacturer declares the
1336 highest use case for which all required capabilities are met.

1337 NOTE: Use case selection is based on the capabilities of the boot manager product. The deployment context of
1338 the device in which the boot manager is integrated is the responsibility of the device manufacturer's
1339 product conformity assessment, not of the boot manager manufacturer.

1340 5 Technical requirements for products

1341 5.1 Introduction: Applicability of the requirements

1342 5.1.1 General

1343 The technical requirements of the present document apply under the product context described in clause 4, which shall
1344 be in accordance with its intended use. The product shall comply with all applicable technical requirements of the
1345 present document at all times when operating in such a product context.

1346 This clause establishes cybersecurity requirements implementing the essential cybersecurity requirements of Annex I,
1347 Part I of Regulation (EU) 2024/2847 [i.1].


1348 The risk analysis underlying the requirements in clause 5 is documented in Annex B and capability‑based conditions
1349 defined in clause 4.2.3. Annex B identifies assets requiring protection (clause B.1), risk factors (clause B.2),
1350 assumptions (clause B.3), threats (clause B.4), and maps risk factors to use cases (clause B.5) and security profiles
1351 (clause B.6).

1352 Not all requirements are universally applicable: The applicability of requirements may be based on use cases or specific
1353 capabilities of the product. Requirements apply based on:

1354 a) the security profile associated with the declared use case (clause 4.8); and

1355 b) implemented functional capabilities: requirements that reference a functional capability apply when that
1356 capability is present, regardless of the declared use case.

1357 Both conditions are evaluated independently. A requirement applies when the security profile condition and the
1358 capability condition (if any) are both met.

1359 5.1.2 Use case declaration

1360 5.1.2.0 Introduction

1361 The manufacturer shall declare the use case (UC-IMM, UC-VER, or UC-HW) applicable to the boot manager product.
1362 The declared use case shall be consistent with the capability requirements defined in Table 4.6.4-1.

1363 5.1.2.1 [UC-IMM]

1364 Where the boot manager is declared as UC-IMM, the boot manager shall meet all of the following conditions:

1365 a) the boot manager executable code and trust anchors reside in hardware-enforced immutable storage;

1366 b) the boot manager does not implement update capability;

1367 c) the boot manager does not implement network boot.

1368 5.1.2.2 [UC-VER]

1369 Where the boot manager is declared as UC-VER, the boot manager shall implement:

1370 a) verified boot as defined in clause 4.2.1.2;

1371 b) update capability as defined in clause 4.2.3.2;

1372 c) logging capability as defined in clause 4.2.3.7;

1373 d) key provisioning as defined in clause 4.2.3.10.

1374 5.1.2.3 [UC-HW]

1375 Where the boot manager is declared as UC-HW, the boot manager shall, in addition to meeting [UC-VER], implement
1376 hardware-backed security mechanisms for key storage and cryptographic operations that protect the root of trust against
1377 remote software attacks.

1378 5.1.3 Not applicable verdict

1379 A requirement verdict of NOT APPLICABLE shall be justified only when all of the following conditions are met:

1380 a) the capability referenced in the requirement's applicability statement is not implemented by the product;

1381 b) the declared use case shown in Table 4.6.4-1 does not assume that capability;

1382 c) residual risks arising from the absence of that capability are documented in the product technical
1383 documentation.


1384 5.2 Appropriate level of cybersecurity

1385 5.2.0 Introduction appropriate level of cybersecurity requirements

1386 This clause addresses the requirements in the CRA [i.1] Annex I, Part I, point (1). The present clause specifies the
1387 cybersecurity risk assessment recorded with the boot manager product and the relationship between the risk assessment
1388 and the requirements in clauses 5.3 to 5.15.

1389 NOTE: Guidance on the generic security framework underlying "appropriate level of cybersecurity" is given in
1390 prEN 40000-1-2 [i.5]. prEN 40000-1-2 is not a normative reference of the present document.

1391 5.2.1 [REQ-BM-RRM-001]

1392 Requirement: The boot manager shall be placed on the market with a cybersecurity risk assessment recorded in the
1393 product technical documentation. The risk assessment shall identify the boot manager product and version assessed, the
1394 methodology used, the scope of the assessment, the assumptions from clause B.3 applicable to the product, the
1395 identified risks, and the conclusions.

1396 Applicability: Applies to all boot managers (all security profiles).

1397 5.2.2 [REQ-BM-RRM-002]

1398 Requirement: The cybersecurity risk assessment shall consider the threats catalogued in Annex B as applicable to the
1399 boot manager's declared use case (clause 4.8) and operational environment (clause 4.3). The identified risks shall be
1400 consistent with the declared use case.

1401 Applicability: Applies to all boot managers (all security profiles).

1402 5.2.3 [REQ-BM-RRM-003]

1403 Requirement: Where the cybersecurity risk assessment identifies risks not addressed by the requirements in clauses 5.3
1404 to 5.15 applicable to the declared use case, additional product-level measures addressing those risks shall be
1405 implemented and documented in the product technical documentation.

1406 Applicability: Applies to all boot managers (all security profiles).

1407 5.2.4 [REQ-BM-RRM-004]

1408 Requirement: Where the boot manager relies on security functions provided by integrated hardware or software
1409 components (see clause 4.4.3), the product technical documentation shall identify the security needs the boot manager
1410 places on those components and shall record documentary or test evidence that the components meet those needs.

1411 Applicability: Applies to boot managers that rely on security functions provided by integrated components.

1412 NOTE: Examples of security functions provided by integrated components include hardware-backed key storage,
1413 cryptographic primitives provided by a secure element, secure boot anchors provided by hardware ROM,
1414 and attack-resistance properties provided by a secure microcontroller or tamper-resistant security
1415 processor.

1416 5.2.5 [REQ-BM-RRM-005]

1417 Requirement: The cybersecurity risk assessment, the mapping of identified risks to the requirements applicable under
1418 clauses 5.3 to 5.15, any additional measures implemented under REQ-BM-RRM-003, and the supporting evidence for
1419 components under REQ-BM-RRM-004, shall be recorded in the product technical documentation.

1420 Applicability: Applies to all boot managers (all security profiles).


1421 5.3 No known exploitable vulnerabilities

1422 5.3.0 Introduction to no known exploitable vulnerabilities requirements

1423 This clause addresses the requirements in the CRA [i.1] Annex I, Part I, point (2)(a). Boot managers shall be placed on
1424 the market without known exploitable vulnerabilities. Due to the pre-OS execution environment, boot manager
1425 vulnerability management relies on verified update mechanisms rather than runtime scanning or automatic updates
1426 triggered on first use.

1427 5.3.1 [REQ-BM-KEV-001]

1428 Requirement: The boot manager shall not be placed on the market with known exploitable vulnerabilities in its code,
1429 configuration, or cryptographic mechanisms.

1430 Applicability: Applies to all boot managers (all security profiles).

1431 5.3.2 [REQ-BM-KEV-002]

1432 Requirement: Where the boot manager implements update capability, security updates addressing known exploitable
1433 vulnerabilities shall be available for the duration of the support period.

1434 Applicability: Applies to boot managers with update capability (MEDIUM or HIGH security profile).

1435 5.3.3 [REQ-BM-KEV-003]

1436 Requirement: Where the boot manager does not implement update capability, the product technical documentation
1437 shall state the absence of update capability as a residual risk, the date of the last vulnerability assessment performed
1438 prior to placing the product on the market, and the defence-in-depth measures implemented to mitigate the inability to
1439 remediate post-deployment vulnerabilities.

1440 Applicability: Applies to boot managers without update capability (LOW security profile).

1441 NOTE: For a boot manager without update capability, vulnerabilities disclosed between the date of the pre-
1442 market vulnerability assessment and the date of placing on the market cannot be remediated through post-
1443 deployment updates. The currency of the assessment is therefore a factor in the residual risk that the
1444 defence-in-depth measures required by this clause are intended to address.

1445 5.4 Secure by default configuration

1446 5.4.0 Introduction to secure by default configuration requirements

1447 This clause addresses the requirements in the CRA [i.1], Annex I, Part I, point (2)(b). Boot managers shall be put on the
1448 market with security features enabled, no default credentials, and protection matching available hardware and software
1449 resources.

1450 NOTE: The technical implementation of security features (i.e. digital signature validation, or authenticated hash
1451 verification, logging capabilities, rollback protection) and the responsibility for enabling them by default
1452 may vary depending on the deployment model, device capabilities, and security policy. In many cases, the
1453 OEM or system integrator is responsible for activating these features during device configuration, rather
1454 than the silicon supplier, that is in charge to provide those features.

1455 5.4.1 [REQ-BM-SBD-001]

1456 Requirement: The boot manager shall enable cryptographic validation by default.

1457 Applicability: Applies to boot managers with verified boot (MEDIUM or HIGH security profile).

1458 NOTE: See clause 5.4.0 for details on implementation and activation details responsibilities.


1459 5.4.2 [REQ-BM-SBD-002]

1460 Requirement: The boot manager shall not implement unauthorised or undocumented mechanisms that allow bypassing
1461 authentication or security controls.

1462 Applicability: Applies to all boot managers (all security profiles).

1463 NOTE: Examples of such mechanisms include:

1464 a) default or hardcoded passwords;

1465 b) maintenance or service backdoors;

1466 c) undocumented access interfaces or commands.

1467 5.4.3 [REQ-BM-SBD-003]

1468 Requirement: Where the boot manager supports password authentication to protect interfaces that can compromise
1469 core security guarantees of the system, the boot manager shall support the establishment of such passwords during
1470 initial deployment.

1471 Applicability: Applies to boot managers with configuration capability using password authentication (all security
1472 profiles).

1473 NOTE: The boot manager provides the mechanism. Whether a password is set at initial deployment depends on
1474 the integrator's configuration of the product.

1475 5.4.4 [REQ-BM-SBD-004]

1476 Requirement: The boot manager shall prevent automatic fallback to a boot mode that disables or weakens verification
1477 or rollback protection without explicit authorisation.

1478 Applicability: Applies to boot managers with verified or measured boot (MEDIUM or HIGH security profile).

1479 5.4.5 [REQ-BM-SBD-005]

1480 Requirement: The boot manager shall provide a prominent indication appropriate for the device's interface capabilities
1481 (e.g. user-visible message, audio notification, LED signal, or logged event) when a security-relevant protection is
1482 reduced or disabled.

1483 Applicability: Applies to boot managers with configuration capability (all profiles).

1484 5.4.6 [REQ-BM-SBD-006]

1485 Requirement: The boot manager shall support restoring the system to secure default configuration settings.

1486 Applicability: Applies to boot managers with configuration capability (all profiles).

1487 NOTE 1: Where configuration or keys are immutable (e.g. ROM/fuses), restoration may be limited. Such
1488 limitations should be documented.

1489 NOTE 2: This requirement applies to boot manager configuration settings. Firmware versioning is governed
1490 separately by the anti-rollback requirements in clause 5.8 (REQ-BM-INT-016, REQ-BM-INT-017);
1491 device-level reset is outside the scope of this requirement.


1492 5.5 Secure updates

1493 5.5.0 Introduction to secure updates requirements

1494 This clause addresses the requirements in the CRA [i.1], Annex I, Part I, point (2)(c). Where update capability is
1495 implemented, boot managers authenticate update sources, protect update-related keys, and isolate update logic from the
1496 normal boot path.

1497 A boot manager assures the integrity and authenticity of an update in one of a small number of ways. The requirements
1498 in this clause apply across all of them. The following implementation scenarios, referred to in the present document as
1499 scenarios A to E, characterise these approaches:

1500  Scenario A: signature verification on the product: the boot manager verifies a digital signature over the update
1501 package, or over signed metadata covering it, before installation. This is the primary approach for boot
1502 managers able to perform public-key verification against a protected trust anchor.

1503  Scenario B: integrity-only verification: the boot manager verifies only an integrity value such as a hash or
1504 checksum and does not itself enforce source authenticity. This approach is confined to constrained boot
1505 managers that cannot perform signature verification, and relies on compensating controls such as a restricted
1506 update interface or an authenticated delivery channel.

1507  Scenario C: authenticated delivery channel: integrity and source authenticity are assured by an authenticated
1508 channel between the boot manager and an authorised update source. This approach applies where updates are
1509 delivered over a network and the boot manager does not perform package signature verification itself.

1510  Scenario D: restricted local or offline update: updates are applied through controlled local mechanisms, for
1511 example by authorised personnel using trusted tooling and controlled media. This is a primary approach where
1512 network update is not available or not permitted.

1513  Scenario E: platform-managed verification: signature verification and installation control are performed by a
1514 trusted underlying platform component rather than by the boot manager. This applies in the narrow case where
1515 the boot manager is updated through such a platform. A boot manager may combine more than one scenario,
1516 for example signed packages delivered over an authenticated channel.

1517 NOTE: Protection against version downgrade and unauthorised rollback of updates is addressed by the anti-
1518 rollback requirements in clause 5.8 (REQ-BM-INT-016, REQ-BM-INT-017).

1519 5.5.1 [REQ-BM-SU-001]

1520 Requirement: Where a user interface is available, the boot manager shall provide status indicators for available updates
1521 or rely on authorised components to present such indicators.

1522 Applicability: Applies to boot managers with update and configuration capabilities (MEDIUM or HIGH security
1523 profile).

1524 NOTE: Authorised components may include the operating system, firmware management services, or other
1525 trusted update-management mechanisms.

1526 5.5.2 [REQ-BM-SU-002]

1527 Requirement: When network capability is available, the boot manager shall support mechanisms to check for the
1528 availability of updates or rely on authorised components to do so.

1529 Applicability: Applies to boot managers with update capability (MEDIUM or HIGH security profile).

1530 NOTE: Authorised components may include the operating system, firmware management services, or other
1531 trusted update-management mechanisms.


1532 5.5.3 [REQ-BM-SU-003]

1533 Requirement: The boot manager shall enable security policy updates through configuration, allow disabling features to
1534 address vulnerabilities, and maintain configuration update capability throughout the support period.

1535 Applicability: Applies to boot managers with configuration capability (all profiles).

1536 5.5.4 [REQ-BM-SU-004]

1537 Requirement: The boot manager shall accept updates only from authenticated and authorised sources.

1538 Applicability: Applies to boot managers with update capability where the boot manager performs on-update
1539 verification (MEDIUM or HIGH security profile).

1540 5.5.5 [REQ-BM-SU-005]

1541 Requirement: When hardware security components are available, the boot manager shall store update-verification keys
1542 using those components.

1543 Applicability: Applies to boot managers with update capability where the boot manager performs on-update
1544 verification (MEDIUM or HIGH security profile).

1545 5.5.6 [REQ-BM-SU-006]

1546 Requirement: The boot manager shall isolate update logic from normal boot path.

1547 Applicability: Applies to boot managers with update capability where the boot manager performs on-update
1548 verification (MEDIUM or HIGH security profile).

1549 5.5.7 [REQ-BM-SU-007]

1550 Requirement: The boot manager shall apply updates atomically, such that an interrupted or failed update does not
1551 leave the boot manager in a partially updated state. If an update cannot be completed successfully, the boot manager
1552 shall revert to the previously operational state and remain bootable.

1553 Applicability: Applies to boot managers that apply updates themselves (MEDIUM or HIGH security profile).

1554 NOTE: The previously operational state is the last boot manager image and configuration known to boot
1555 successfully. The mechanism for retaining it (for example a redundant image or a recovery image) is an
1556 implementation choice.

1557 5.6 Authentication and access control

1558 5.6.0 Introduction to authentication and access control requirements

1559 This clause addresses the requirements in the CRA [i.1], Annex I, Part I, point (2)(d). Boot managers control code
1560 execution authorisation and configuration protection through cryptographic verification, physical presence detection,
1561 and hardware-based protections.

1562 5.6.1 [REQ-BM-AC-001]

1563 Requirement: The boot manager shall require that any configuration changes to trusted keys, certificates, or trust
1564 anchor databases are authorised only through one of the following mechanisms:

1565 a) direct physical presence at the device; or

1566 b) submission of a signed artefact whose authenticity and integrity are cryptographically verified by the boot
1567 manager prior to application.
